<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality chromosome-level genome assembly reveals genetics for important traits in eggplant"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-09-21"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Eggplant (Solanum melongena L.) is an economically important vegetable crop in the Solanaceae family, with extensive diversity among landraces and close relatives. Here, we report a high-quality reference genome for the eggplant inbred line HQ-1315 (S. melongena-HQ) using a combination of Illumina, Nanopore and 10X genomics sequencing technologies and Hi-C technology for genome assembly. The assembled genome has a total size of ~1.17&#8201;Gb and 12 chromosomes, with a contig N50 of 5.26&#8201;Mb, consisting of 36,582 protein-coding genes. Repetitive sequences comprise 70.09% (811.14&#8201;Mb) of the eggplant genome, most of which are long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (65.80%), followed by long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs, 1.54%) and DNA transposons (0.85%). The S. melongena-HQ eggplant genome carries a total of 563 accession-specific gene families containing 1009 genes. In total, 73 expanded gene families (892 genes) and 34 contraction gene families (114 genes) were functionally annotated. Comparative analysis of different eggplant genomes identified three types of variations, including single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), insertions/deletions (indels) and structural variants (SVs). Asymmetric SV accumulation was found in potential regulatory regions of protein-coding genes among the different eggplant genomes. Furthermore, we performed QTL-seq for eggplant fruit length using the S. melongena-HQ reference genome and detected a QTL interval of 71.29&#8211;78.26&#8201;Mb on chromosome E03. The gene Smechr0301963, which belongs to the SUN gene family, is predicted to be a key candidate gene for eggplant fruit length regulation. Moreover, we anchored a total of 210 linkage markers associated with 71 traits to the eggplant chromosomes and finally obtained 26 QTL hotspots. The eggplant HQ-1315 genome assembly can be accessed at 
                http://eggplant-hq.cn
                
              . In conclusion, the eggplant genome presented herein provides a global view of genomic divergence at the whole-genome level and powerful tools for the identification of candidate genes for important traits in eggplant."/>

            <li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/plant-evolution" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Plant evolution</a></li><li class="c-article-subject-list__subject"><a href="/subjects/structural-variation" data-track="click" data-track-action="view subject" data-track-label="link" itemprop="about">Structural variation</a></li>
        </ul>
    </div>

                
    

    

            </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Eggplant (<i>Solanum melongena</i> L.) is an economically important vegetable crop in the Solanaceae family, with extensive diversity among landraces and close relatives. Here, we report a high-quality reference genome for the eggplant inbred line HQ-1315 (<i>S. melongena</i>-HQ) using a combination of Illumina, Nanopore and 10X genomics sequencing technologies and Hi-C technology for genome assembly. The assembled genome has a total size of ~1.17 Gb and 12 chromosomes, with a contig N50 of 5.26 Mb, consisting of 36,582 protein-coding genes. Repetitive sequences comprise 70.09% (811.14 Mb) of the eggplant genome, most of which are long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (65.80%), followed by long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs, 1.54%) and DNA transposons (0.85%). The <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ eggplant genome carries a total of 563 accession-specific gene families containing 1009 genes. In total, 73 expanded gene families (892 genes) and 34 contraction gene families (114 genes) were functionally annotated. Comparative analysis of different eggplant genomes identified three types of variations, including single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), insertions/deletions (indels) and structural variants (SVs). Asymmetric SV accumulation was found in potential regulatory regions of protein-coding genes among the different eggplant genomes. Furthermore, we performed QTL-seq for eggplant fruit length using the <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ reference genome and detected a QTL interval of 71.29–78.26 Mb on chromosome E03. The gene <i>Smechr0301963</i>, which belongs to the <i>SUN</i> gene family, is predicted to be a key candidate gene for eggplant fruit length regulation. Moreover, we anchored a total of 210 linkage markers associated with 71 traits to the eggplant chromosomes and finally obtained 26 QTL hotspots. The eggplant HQ-1315 genome assembly can be accessed at <a href="http://eggplant-hq.cn">http://eggplant-hq.cn</a>. In conclusion, the eggplant genome presented herein provides a global view of genomic divergence at the whole-genome level and powerful tools for the identification of candidate genes for important traits in eggplant.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The large family Solanaceae contains over 3000 plant species that are adapted to a wide range of geographic conditions, including eggplant (<i>Solanum melongena</i>), tomato (<i>S. lycopersicum</i>), potato (<i>S. tuberosum</i>), tobacco (<i>Nicotiana tabacum</i>) and petunia (<i>Petunia inflata</i>). Asian eggplant (<i>S. melongena</i> L.), also known as brinjal or aubergine, is a vegetable crop widely grown across Southeast Asian, African, and Mediterranean countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Chapman, M. A. The Eggplant Genome. (Springer Nature Switzerland AG, 2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16122e440">1</a></sup>. Eggplant is the third most widely grown solanaceous vegetable after potatoes and tomatoes, with a global total production of ~54.08 million tons in 2018 (FAOSTAT; <a href="http://faostat3.fao.org">http://faostat3.fao.org</a>). Approximately 90% of eggplants are produced in Asia, mainly in China and India, with Indonesia, Turkey, Egypt, the Philippines and Iran growing ~1% of the world’s total production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Chapman, M. A. The Eggplant Genome. (Springer Nature Switzerland AG, 2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16122e451">1</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="World production and diversity of eggplants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: World production and diversity of eggplants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="787" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of worldwide eggplant production according to FAOSTAT in 2018. <b>b</b> Diversity in fruit morphology among different eggplants</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Unlike tomato and potato, which are both New World representatives of the genus <i>Solanum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Weese, T. L. &amp; Bohs, L. Eggplant origins: out of Africa, into the Orient. Taxon 59, 49–56 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d16122e488">2</a></sup>, eggplant is an Old World crop belonging to subgenus <i>Leptostemonum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Daunay, M. C. et al. Genetic resources of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.) and allied species: a new challenge for molecular geneticists and eggplant breeders. Solanaceae V., Advances in Taxonomy and Utilization. (Nijmegen University Press, pp. 251–274, Nijmegen, The Netherlands, 2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d16122e494">3</a></sup> (the “spiny solanums”). Two other <i>Solanum</i> species, Ethiopian/scarlet eggplant (<i>S. aethiopicum</i> L.) and African/Gboma eggplant (<i>S. macrocarpon</i> L.), are also called eggplants, and their fruits and leaves are used for food and medicine. There are obvious local preferences for eggplant fruits, which may be either elongated or round, with colors from dark purple to light green. The domestication history of eggplant has been under debate and presumably started in Africa, with radiation to Asia; however, relationships among the African species and their Asian relatives are not well resolved<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Meyer, R. S., Karol, K. G., Little, D. P., Nee, M. H. &amp; Litt, A. Phylogeographic relationships among Asian eggplants and new perspectives on eggplant domestication. Mol. Phylogenetics Evol. 63, 685–701 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d16122e508">4</a></sup>. The two most commonly hypothesized regions of origin are India and southern China/Southeast (SE) Asia, which have equally old written records of eggplant use for ~2000 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Meyer, R. S., Karol, K. G., Little, D. P., Nee, M. H. &amp; Litt, A. Phylogeographic relationships among Asian eggplants and new perspectives on eggplant domestication. Mol. Phylogenetics Evol. 63, 685–701 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d16122e512">4</a></sup>. Both regions have vastly diverse landraces, close wild relatives and candidate progenitors of eggplant. A recent study proposed that <i>S. insanum</i> is the wild progenitor, which split into an Eastern and Western group, with domesticates derived from the Eastern group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Page, A., Gibson, J., Meyer, R. S. &amp; Chapman, M. A. Eggplant domestication: pervasive gene flow, feralization, and transcriptomic divergence. Mol. Biol. Evol. 36, 1359–1372 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d16122e519">5</a></sup>. Eggplants exhibit highly diverse variations in growth habits, biotic and abiotic resistance, and fruit and leaf morphology among local landraces and wild relatives. Identification of candidate genes/gene families controlling these differences will provide insight into the genetic mechanisms of agronomically important traits, as well as resources for eggplant breeding.</p><p>Genome sequencing is a powerful tool in plant genetics and genomics research. The genome of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> was sequenced and published in 2000, representing the first plant genome. Since then, the development of genome sequencing technologies has resulted in multitude of plant genomes in recent years, including those of many horticultural crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657–666 (2016)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_3">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_4">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270–278 (2014)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_5">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qin, C. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of cultivated and wild peppers provides insights into Capsicum domestication and specialization. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 5135–5140 (2014)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_6">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649–660 (2014)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_7">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, D. L. et al. Draft genome sequence of cauliflower (Brassica oleracea L. var. botrytis) provides new insights into the C genome in Brassica species. Hortic. Res. 6, 82 (2019)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d16122e529_8">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li, M. Y. et al. The genome sequence of celery (Apium graveolens L.), an important leaf vegetable crop rich in apigenin in the Apiaceae family. Hortic. Res. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41438-019-0235-2&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16122e532">15</a></sup>. Traditionally, the majority of research in <i>Solanum</i> crops has focused on potato and tomato, for which genomes have been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16122e539">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16122e542">10</a></sup>. The first genome sequence of <i>S. melongena</i> was published in 2014, with 85,446 predicted genes and an N50 of 64 kb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649–660 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16122e550">13</a></sup>. However, this draft assembly is not at the chromosome level and is highly fragmented, containing 33,873 scaffolds and covering only 74% of the eggplant genome. An improved <i>S. melongena</i> genome of the inbred line 67/3 using Illumina sequencing and single-molecule optical mapping was then published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Barchi, L. et al. A chromosome-anchored eggplant genome sequence reveals key events in Solanaceae evolution. Sci. Rep. 9, 11769 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16122e557">16</a></sup>. In addition, the genome of the African eggplant <i>S. aethiopicum</i>, a close relative of <i>S. melongena</i>, has also been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Song, B. et al. Draft genome sequence of Solanum aethiopicum provides insights into disease resistance, drought tolerance, and the evolution of the genome. GigaScience 8, 1–16 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16122e567">17</a></sup>. However, these eggplant genomes were all sequenced with next-generation sequencing (NGS) technologies using short reads, whereas genome sequence data derived from third-generation sequencing with long reads are still not publicly available. Here, we report a high-quality chromosome-level eggplant genome using next-generation Illumina sequencing and third-generation Nanopore sequencing combined with 10X genomic and Hi-C technologies, with a contig N50 of 5.26 Mb and a scaffold N50 of 89.64 Mb.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly, and assessment</h3><p>The genome size of the eggplant inbred line HQ-1315 is ~1205.25 Mb, with a heterozygosity rate of 0.15%, as assessed by k-mer analysis based on 93.33 Gb Illumina HiSeq data. The estimated proportion of repeat sequences was ~69.60%.</p><p>A high-quality eggplant genome (hereafter <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ) was assembled with a genome size of ~1.1 Gb and contig N50 of 5.26 Mb. We used a combination of Illumina HiSeq, Nanopore sequencing, and 10X Genomics sequencing technologies to sequence and assemble the eggplant genome; with the assistance of the Hi-C technique, a chromosome-level genome assembly was generated. A total of 114.45 Gb reads were obtained from Illumina HiSeq, including 93.33 Gb data for k-mer analysis and 21.12 Gb of additional read data, the average coverage of which was 94.96×; Nanopore sequencing generated 129 Gb data with 107.03× coverage. These data were used for preliminary assembly, producing a total contig length of 1159.53 Mb and a contig N50 of 5.71 Mb; the total scaffold length is 1159.53 Mb, with a scaffold N50 of 5.71 Mb. Then, we added ~113.46 Gb 10X Genomics data (~94.14×) for further assembly, resulting in a modified eggplant genome version with a contig length of 1,152.97 Mb and contig N50 of 5.75 Mb. The scaffold length is 1,157.36 Mb, with a scaffold N50 of 9.79 Mb, which is a 1.71-fold increase compared to the genome version by Hirakawa et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649–660 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16122e589">13</a></sup>. Finally, with the assistance of 131.73 Gb Hi-C reads, the assembled scaffold N50 reached 89.64 Mb, with a final contig N50 of 5.26 Mb. Twelve pseudochromosomes with a total length of 1,173.14 Mb were obtained, accounting for 92.72% of the estimated eggplant genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig2">2</a>; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Tab1">1</a>). Detailed information on the stepwise assembly of the genome is shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM6">S1</a>. The GC content in the eggplant genome is 35.94%, similar to that of <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d16122e605">18</a></sup> (36.06%), tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16122e609">10</a></sup> (34.05%) and celery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li, M. Y. et al. The genome sequence of celery (Apium graveolens L.), an important leaf vegetable crop rich in apigenin in the Apiaceae family. Hortic. Res. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41438-019-0235-2&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16122e613">15</a></sup> (35.35%) but lower than that of rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d16122e617">19</a></sup> (43.57%) and tea plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d16122e621">20</a></sup> (42.31%).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Circos diagram of different elements on eggplant chromosomes. E01-E12 refers to the 12 assembled chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Circos diagram of different elements on eggplant chromosomes. E01-E12 refers to the 12 assembled chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="682" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Assembled chromosomes (Mb). <b>b</b> Gene density. <b>c</b> GC content. <b>d</b> Transposon density. <b>e</b> Tandem repeat density. <b>f</b> Syntenic blocks</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the three published eggplant genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The quality of the eggplant genome assembly was further assessed (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM1">S1</a>). The alignment rate of all short reads to the genome was ~99.48%, covering 91.24% of the genome. The heterozygous and homozygous SNP ratios were calculated to be 0.0253% and 0.0014%, respectively, indicating a high single-base accuracy rate for the genome assembly. The integrity of the assembled genome was assessed by the Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA); 237 genes were assembled from 248 core eukaryotic genes (CEGs), accounting for 95.56% of the total and reflecting that the sequence assembly was relatively complete. The statistical results of BUSCO evaluation of the eggplant genome showed that 2,190 homologous single-copy genes were assembled and that 94.2% of all single-copy genes were assembled.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>For the annotation of the eggplant genome, we used a combination of gene prediction strategies, including de novo, homology, and transcriptome-based predictions. RNA from five different tissues, including root, stem, leaf, flower and fruit, was extracted for next-generation transcriptome sequencing and full-length transcriptome sequencing. A total of 36,582 coding genes were predicted, with an average of 4.31 exons per gene and an average transcript length of 4095.69 bp. Repetitive sequence annotation results showed that 70.09% of the eggplant genome is repeat sequences, with a size of 811.14 Mb. Most of the repeat sequences are long terminal repeat (LTR)-type retrotransposons, which account for 65.80%; 1.54% is the long interspersed nuclear element (LINE) type, and DNA transposons account for only 0.85%. In addition, 5929 noncoding RNAs were detected in the eggplant genome, including 268 miRNAs with an average length of 127.81 bp as well as 2549 tRNAs, and 554 snRNAs (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM7">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of the <i>S. melongena</i> genome</h3><p>A total of 9 sequenced Solanaceae genomes were analyzed to reveal the evolution of the eggplant genome, including <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i>, <i>Capsicum annuum</i>, <i>Petunia inflata</i>, <i>S. tuberosum</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>S. aethiopicum</i>, <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ, and two other <i>S. melongena</i> genomes, <i>S. melongena</i>-NS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649–660 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16122e967">13</a></sup> and <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Barchi, L. et al. A chromosome-anchored eggplant genome sequence reveals key events in Solanaceae evolution. Sci. Rep. 9, 11769 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16122e975">16</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analysis indicated that eggplant is closer to potato and tomato than pepper (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3a</a>), diverging from the common ancestor ~14.4 Mya (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM2">2</a>). The group of three <i>Solanum</i> species (eggplant, potato and tomato) is sister to pepper, diverging ~18.5 Mya. Among the different eggplants, <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and its close relative <i>S. aethiopicum</i> diverged from a common ancestor ~2.4 Mya (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM2">2</a>). Moreover, <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ is more closely related to the European eggplant variety <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 than the Japanese eggplant cultivar <i>S. melongena</i>-NS, all of which are distant from <i>S. aethiopicum</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparative analysis of the S.melongena-HQ genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Comparative analysis of the <i>S.melongena</i>-HQ genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="556" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree of 9 sequenced Solanaceae genomes. <b>b</b> Distribution of genes in different species. The horizontal axis indicates the analyzed genome, and the vertical axis indicates the number of corresponding genes. Pink represents the number of single-copy orthologs. Orange represents multiple-copy orthologs, olive green the unique genes of the corresponding genome, and green the number of other orthologs. <b>c</b> Venn diagram of the common and unique gene families among different eggplant genomes. <b>d</b> Gene family expansion and contraction in the 9 Solanaceae genomes. The green numbers indicate expanded gene families, and the red numbers represent contracted gene families</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There were 32,529 gene families in total according to clustering results. Among the nine genomes, 6087 gene families are common, of which 463 single-copy gene families are common to each genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3b</a>). The corresponding clustering results for <i>S. melongena</i>-NS, <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3, <i>S. aethiopicum,</i> and <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ were extracted to draw a Venn diagram, which showed that the four eggplant genomes have 11,123 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3c</a>). Compared with other eggplants, <i>S. melongena</i>-NS has the most unique genes (1,256 genes), followed by <i>S. aethiopicum</i> with 1226 unique genes; <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 has only 295 unique genes. In addition, <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ has a total of 563 accession-specific gene families containing 1009 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3c</a>, Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM8">3</a>). We performed GO and KEGG enrichment analyses on accession-specific gene families of <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM8">3</a>) and found them to be mainly involved in the processes of metabolism, biosynthesis and modification of proteins/nucleic acids.</p><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) events in the <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ genome were detected based on the rate of fourfold degenerative third-codon transversion (4DTv) of paralogous gene pairs among <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ, <i>A. thaliana</i> and four other Solanaceae species. As illustrated in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig4">4</a>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ had one peak value at ~0.72, indicating an ancient WGD before the divergence of asterids and rosids. <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ had only one WGD event common to Solanaceae species at ~0.30, whereas there was no recent WGD after species differentiation. Among Solanaceae crops, <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ first diverged from pepper at ~0.1, followed by tomato at ~0.08, and then <i>S. tuberosum</i> at ~0.06. The two eggplants <i>S. aethiopicum</i> and <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ diverged from each other quite recently compared with other species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Distribution of 4DTv distances."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Distribution of 4DTv distances.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="455" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The <i>x</i>-axis indicates the 4DTv distance. The <i>y</i>-axis indicates the percentage of gene pairs</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expansion and contraction of gene families</h3><p>The 9 sequenced Solanaceae genomes were analyzed to reveal the dynamics of gene family evolution in the eggplant genome. A total of 32,522 most recent common ancestor (MRCA) gene families were found (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3d</a>). Compared with their ancestors six gene families expanded and 23 gene families contracted in <i>S. melongena</i> and <i>S. aethiopicum</i>. Among the different eggplant genomes, <i>S. melongena</i>-NS has 539 gene families that significantly expanded and 38 gene families that contracted, whereas <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 has 80 expanded gene families and 76 contracted gene families. <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ has 73 expanded gene families, including 892 genes, and 34 contracted gene families, including 114 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig3">3d</a>, Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM9">4</a>). The expanded and contracted genes were also annotated by GO and KEGG analyses (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM9">4</a>). The KEGG pathway plant-pathogen interaction showed the most contracted genes (25 genes), which may be related to reduced resistance in cultivated eggplant.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Comparative genomic analysis</h3><p>Synteny analysis showed that the <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ genome exhibits high collinearity with that of <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3, with a total of 19,620 gene pairs and 178 syntenic blocks. Chromosome E01 in these two eggplant genomes is in the same direction but inverted compared with tomato chromosome 1. There is one missing block in <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 chromosome E02, which exists between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and tomato and between tomato and pepper. Similar missing segments were also found for corresponding chromosomes 5 and 9. Chromosomes 4, 5, 10, 11, and 12 have undergone more complex chromosome rearrangements, such as translocations and inversions, during evolution among eggplant, tomato and pepper, as reflected by an increased number of syntenic blocks. We identified a total of 18,337 gene pairs and 151 syntenic blocks between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and tomato. <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ chromosome E04 was partly aligned to tomato chromosomes 4, 10 and 11; some of the genes on <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ chromosome E05 were aligned to tomato chromosome 12. Genes from <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ chromosome E10 were aligned to <i>S. lycopersicum</i> chromosomes 3, 5 and 12. Similar collinearity was also detected among the genes from corresponding chromosomes 11 and 12 between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig5">5</a>). Pairwise comparisons are presented in Supplementary Figs. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM3">3</a>–S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM5">5</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Synteny analysis of genes in C. annuum, S. lycopersicum, S. melongena-67/3, and S. melongena-HQ."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Synteny analysis of genes in <i>C. annuum</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>S. melongena-</i>67/3, and <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="450" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The numbers indicate the corresponding chromosomes in each species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although the overall genome lengths of <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 are not significantly different, the length of each chromosome differ significantly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Tab2">2</a>). The total sizes of the two eggplant genomes are 1073.14 and 1142.80 Mb, respectively, with a total size difference of 69.66 Mb. The largest difference is with regard to chromosome E09; the length of E09 in <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ is 89.64 Mb, whereas that of <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 is only 36.10 Mb, with a difference of 53.54 Mb. The smallest difference was found for E03, with a difference of only 0.30 Mb, followed by E02, with a difference of only 7.92 Mb. The length of E05 in <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ is 37.74 Mb longer than that in <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3, and the length of <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ E07 is 35.59 Mb shorter than that of <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3. The differences in the lengths of other chromosomes, E04, E08, E06, E10, E11, and E12, are between 19.30 and 28.93 Mb. Despite the minor differences in total genome size between the two assembled eggplant genome versions themselves, the differences in chromosome length between the two assembled versions are significant. This result may have been caused by different sequencing technologies (second vs third generation) and assembly strategies (linkage map vs Hi-C).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Comparison of chromosome lengths between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We then compared <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ with two previously sequenced eggplant genomes, those of European eggplant <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 and African eggplant <i>S. aethiopicum</i>, to investigate genomic divergence among them (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig6">6a</a>). Three types of variations were analysed, including single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), insertions/deletions (indels) and structural variants (SVs). We detected 2,189,112 SNPs, 512,849 indels, and 741 large SVs between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3. In contrast, 22,092,994 SNPs, 1,988,560 indels, and 7,362 large SVs were identified between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. aethiopicum</i>. Between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3, the 512,849 indel mutations involve 14,756 genes, which were annotated using GO and KEGG (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM10">5</a>). The 741 SVs correspond to 211 genes, among which 60 were functionally enriched by GO analyses (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM10">5</a>). For <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. aethiopicum</i>, 3,066 genes are associated with large SVs, among which 1,370 and 350 genes were functionally enriched according to GO and KEGG analysis, respectively (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM11">6</a>). There are 90 genes involved in antibiotic biosynthesis networks according to the KEGG enrichment results, and 16 genes related to the citrate cycle (TCA cycle). It has been proposed that the African eggplant <i>S. aethiopicum</i> has better disease resistance and drought tolerance than cultivated <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Song, B. et al. Draft genome sequence of Solanum aethiopicum provides insights into disease resistance, drought tolerance, and the evolution of the genome. GigaScience 8, 1–16 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16122e1738">17</a></sup>. Therefore, these genes will provide valuable resources for resistance improvement in eggplant breeding.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="The variation analysis among S. melongena-HQ, S. melongena-67/3 and S. aethiopicum"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: The variation analysis among <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ, <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 and <i>S. aethiopicum</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="334" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Asymmetric SV accumulation among different eggplants. The tracks (from outside to inside) indicate chromosomes, SNP density, indel density, and percentage of SV length. <b>b</b> SV variation percentages in potential regulatory regions of protein-coding genes. The horizontal axis indicates up- and downstream gene regions, and the vertical axis indicates the variation percentage. Pink represents the number of single-copy orthologs. Purple and green lines indicate SV deletions and insertions between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. aethiopicum</i>, respectively. Blue and yellow lines indicate SV deletions and insertions between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We further investigated SV abundance in potential regulatory regions of protein-coding genes; different types of indel variation suggest different patterns of SV accumulation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig6">6b</a>). There were more deletions than insertions between <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. aethiopicum</i>. However, insertions and deletions between the two <i>S. melongena</i> genomes were similar in both coding and noncoding areas, with the two lines basically coinciding. Higher insertion-deletion variations were observed in transcription start site (TSS) and transcription terminal site (TTS) regions of <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ and <i>S. aethiopicum</i>, and the variation in the gene coding regions was found to be slightly higher than that in noncoding regions. In contrast, variations in coding regions were lower than those in noncoding region between cultivated eggplants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">NBS gene family and transcription factor analysis</h3><p>Nucleotide-binding site-leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) proteins constitute the largest family of resistance (R) proteins and play significant roles in defense against pathogens. The NBS protein family was systematically analysed in five plants of the Solanaceae family. In <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ, 301 NBS genes were identified as involved in seven types (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Tab3">3</a>; Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM12">7</a>), whereas only 250 genes were identified in <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 as involved in eight types. <i>S. aethiopicum</i> has outstanding resistance to various pathogens, including <i>Fusarium</i>, <i>Ralstonia</i> and <i>Verticillium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cécile, C. et al. Source of resistance against Ralstonia solanacearum in fertile somatic hybrids of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.) with Solanum aethiopicum L. Plant Sci. 160, 301–313 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d16122e1842">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Gisbert, C. et al. Eggplant relatives as sources of variation for developing new rootstocks: effects of grafting on eggplant yield and fruit apparent quality and composition. Sci. Horti. 128, 14–22 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d16122e1845">22</a></sup>, with 436 NBS genes involved in ten types. Accordingly, <i>S. aethiopicum</i> has been routinely used to improve disease resistance in <i>S. melongena</i>. <i>S. lycopersicum</i> was found to possess 223 NBS genes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Summary of the NBS gene family</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In terms of transcription factors, for <i>S. melongena-</i>HQ, a total of 1970 transcription factors divided into 64 categories, the top three of which were APETALA2/ethylene responsive factor (AP2/ERF, 150), cysteine 2-histidine 2 type zinc finger gene (C2H2, 137) and basic helix-loop-helix (bHLH, 135) were identified. The v-myb avian myeloblastosis viral oncogene homolog superfamily (MYB) has 127 transcription factors. Detailed information on the number and gene sequences of each transcription factor, including <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3, <i>S. aethiopicum</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, is shown in Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM13">8</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Candidate gene identification for fruit length and QTL hotspots in eggplant</h3><p>Eggplants display extensive variations in fruit morphology among landraces and wild relatives. There are obvious local market preferences for fruit shape (i.e., oval, round or linear) according to different consuming habits; thus, the fruit length, diameter and shape index of eggplants show significant differences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig1">1</a>). The immature fruits of HQ-1315 are generally ~35 cm in length and ~3 cm in diameter, and it is a long (elongated type) eggplant. An F<sub>2</sub> population containing 129 individuals was obtained from a cross between HQ-1315 (P<sub>1</sub>) and the short round eggplant 1815 (P<sub>2</sub>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig7">7</a>). Bulked segregant analysis (BSA) and quantitative trait locus (QTL) analysis on eggplant fruit length were then conducted using the <i>S. melongena</i>-HQ genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig7">7</a>). F<sub>2</sub> plants with extremely long and short fruits were selected and pooled for genome sequencing. Resequencing P<sub>2</sub> generated 23.41 Gb of data, and sequencing of the two extreme pools yielded 41.52 Gb for the extreme long pool and 40.05 Gb for the extreme short pool. The average length (L), diameter (D), and fruit shape index (L/D) of three fruits from each F<sub>2</sub> individual were measured to determine the value for the individual plant (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM14">9</a>). Based on genotyping results, a total of 1,019,131 SNPs and 116,676 indel markers showed homozygous differences between the two parents, and the index of the markers in the two progeny pools compared to those of the parents were analyzed and calculated. According to the Δ(All-Index) value, a QTL interval for fruit length was determined within 71.29–78.26 Mb on eggplant chromosome E03 (99% confidence interval) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig7">7</a>). Combined with the genetic mapping results of our previous study, Marker2384739 and Marker2387171 are linked to QTL <i>FS3.1</i>, the physical locations of which are 77.62 and 79.77 Mb respectively. As suggested by the eggplant-tomato synteny relationship, genes controlling fruit size in tomato are likely to have similar functions in determining eggplant fruit size. We obtained a total of 11 genes homologous to regulators of fruit size on eggplant chromosome E03 via homology comparison. Among them, three genes are within or adjacent to the QTL region on E03: <i>Smechr0301760</i> (72.91 Mb), <i>Smechr0301963</i> (78.39 Mb) and <i>Smechr0302217</i> (82.30 Mb). <i>Smechr0301760</i> is a potentially orthologous gene of the <i>cell number regulator</i> (<i>CNR</i>) gene family, whereas <i>Smechr0301963</i> and <i>Smechr0302217</i> are potentially orthologous genes of the <i>SUN</i> gene family. According to the results of QTL-seq and genetic mapping, we predict that <i>Smechr0301963</i> is a key candidate gene for regulating eggplant fruit length. Moreover, 7 homologs of fruit size-related genes are distributed within 89.89–95.48 Mb region, and they may also play potential roles in controlling fruit size.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="QTL analysis of fruit length trait."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: QTL analysis of fruit length trait.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="200" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b> Fruits of P<sub>1</sub>, P<sub>2</sub>, and F<sub>1</sub>. <b>b</b> F<sub>2</sub> individuals with extreme fruit lengths. <b>c</b> Distribution of Δ (All-index) on chromosomes. The light blue line indicates the results of a replacement test with 1000 replicates; the 95% confidence level was selected as the screening threshold</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Based on the QTL results of previous studies and the available marker sequence information, we anchored these markers to our latest reference genome to investigate QTL hotspots in eggplant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barchi, L. et al. A RAD tag derived marker based eggplant linkage map and the location of QTLs determining anthocyanin pigmentation. PLoS ONE 7, e43740 (2012)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barchi, L. et al. QTL analysis reveals new eggplant loci involved in resistance to fungal wilts. Euphytica 214, 20 (2018)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cericola, F. et al. Linkage disequilibrium and genome-wide association analysis for anthocyanin pigmentation and fruit color in eggplant. BMC Genomics 15, 896 (2014)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315_2">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Portis, E. et al. QTL mapping in eggplant reveals clusters of yield-related loci and orthology with the tomato genome. PLoS ONE 9, e89499 (2014)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315_3">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Portis, E. et al. Association mapping for fruit, plant and leaf morphology traits in eggplant. PLoS ONE 10, e0135200 (2015)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315_4">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Toppino, L. et al. Mapping quantitative trait loci affecting biochemical and morphological fruit properties in eggplant (Solanum melongena L.). Front. Plant Sci. 4, 256 (2016)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315_5">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Miyatake, K. et al. Detailed mapping of a resistance locus against Fusarium wilt in cultivated eggplant (Solanum melongena L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 357–367 (2016)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2315_6">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wei, Q. Z. et al. Construction of a SNP-Based genetic map using SLAF-Seq and QTL analysis of morphological traits in eggplant. Front. Genet. 11, 178 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2318">30</a></sup>. A total of 210 linkage markers related to 71 traits, including fruit-related traits (i.e., fruit size and color), leaf morphology, and nutrient components, were anchored (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig8">8</a>, Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM15">10</a>). Except for the linkage markers for <i>Fusarium</i> resistance in Miyatake et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Miyatake, K. et al. Detailed mapping of a resistance locus against Fusarium wilt in cultivated eggplant (Solanum melongena L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 357–367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2331">29</a></sup>, most of the markers were mapped to physical locations on corresponding chromosomes. We summarized the regions with clustered linkage markers or traits and finally obtained 26 QTL hotspots, with two to three on each chromosome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Distribution of QTL hotspots on 12 eggplant chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8: Distribution of QTL hotspots on 12 eggplant chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig8_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig8_HTML.png" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="716" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>The physical locations and marker names are on the right of each chromosome; names of associated traits are on the left of each chromosome. Red rectangles represent QTL hotspots. The full names of the traits involved and corresponding markers are shown in Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#MOESM15">10</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Eggplant Genome Database</h3><p>We constructed an advanced, intuitive, and user-friendly Eggplant Genome Database using genome assembly and annotation data (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#Fig9">9</a>). Eggplant Genome Database consists of three main modules. The browse module has links to information for 36,582 genes, including start/end locations and chromosome information. KEGG, Pfam, GO, NR, and Swiss-Prot database annotation information can be easily accessed by clicking the gene ID, as can the coding sequence (CDS) and protein sequence information corresponding to each gene. The BLAST module aligns sequences to the genome, gene, and protein databases to obtain the required information for users. The eggplant genome assembly, as well as genome gff, CDS, protein, and other data files, can be downloaded using the download module. Eggplant Genome Database provides access to various types of data, allowing researchers and breeders to browse, search, and download information for genomics studies and breeding. The online database can be accessed at <a href="http://eggplant-hq.cn/">http://eggplant-hq.cn/</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="An overview of Eggplant Genome Database."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9: An overview of Eggplant Genome Database.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig9_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00391-0/MediaObjects/41438_2020_391_Fig9_HTML.png" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="571" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p><b>a</b> Homepage. <b>b</b> Browse module. <b>c</b> Blast module. <b>d</b> Download module</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Genome sequencing technologies have undergone tremendous improvement during the past decades, resulting in substantial advances in the availability of plant genomes. Since the publication of the first plant genome, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, using whole-genome shotgun sequencing, over 200 plant genomes have been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bolger, M. E., Arsova, B. &amp; Usadel, B. Plant genome and transcriptome annotations: from misconceptions to simple solutions. Brief. Bioinform. 19, 437–449 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2419">31</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.plabipd.de">www.plabipd.de</a>). However, genome sequencing of plant species with large genome sizes and high repetitive sequence contents remains difficult<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Jiao, W. B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. The impact of third generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 36, 64–70 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2430">32</a></sup>. Compared with the short reads produced by NGS technologies, long reads with kilobase-length DNA fragments are extremely efficient in resolving repetitive regions and facilitating genome assembly. Several new technologies have been developed based on long reads, such as synthetic long reads, long PacBio reads, and optical mapping, and these methods have been applied to <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2436">33</a></sup>, tomato (3.0 genome release; <a href="http://www.solgenomics.net">www.solgenomics.net</a>) and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700–2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2448">34</a></sup>. Nevertheless, long-read sequencing technologies are still costly and rely on the previous extraction of high-quality DNA. Oxford Nanopore is a recently developed long-read sequencing technology that can greatly reduce the sequencing costs and generate gigabases of sequence data from a single flow cell<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Maximilian, H. W. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336–2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2452">35</a></sup>. Hi-C proximity ligation is another driving technology that may help in the assembly of fragmented plant genomes at the chromosome level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Reyes-Chin-Wo, S. et al. Genome assembly with in vitro proximity ligation data and whole-genome triplication in lettuce. Nat. Commun. 8, 14953 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2456">36</a></sup>. In the present study, we combined 114.45 Gb Illumina short reads with 129 Gb long reads from Nanopore sequencing and ~113.46 Gb 10X Genomics data to generate a high-quality eggplant genome, with a contig N50 of 5.26 Mb and a scaffold N50 of 89.64 Mb. With the assistance of 131.73 Gb Hi-C data, 12 eggplant pseudochromosomes were obtained, with a total size of ~1.07 Gb, covering 92.72% of the eggplant genome. Both contig N50 and scaffold N50 were significantly improved compared with those of previously published <i>S. melongena</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Draft genome sequence of eggplant (Solanum melongena L.): the representative solanum species indigenous to the old world. DNA Res. 21, 649–660 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2463">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Barchi, L. et al. A chromosome-anchored eggplant genome sequence reveals key events in Solanaceae evolution. Sci. Rep. 9, 11769 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2466">16</a></sup>. The number of scaffolds obtained was 10,383 for <i>S. melongena</i>-67/3 and 33,873 for <i>S. melongena</i>-NS; we assembled 2,263 scaffolds. A total of 36,582 protein-coding genes were detected in the present study, similar to the ~35,000 genes annotated in other sequenced diploid Solanaceae genomes.</p><p>Eggplant belongs to the genus <i>Solanum</i> and the family Solanaceae, which comprises over 3000 species adapted to a wide range of environments, including nine with sequenced genomes, i.e., potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="The Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2483">9</a></sup>, tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Tomato genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2487">10</a></sup>, pepper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270–278 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00391-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d16122e2491">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Qin, C. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of cultivated and wild peppers provides insights into Capsicum domestication and specialization. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci.
